0553386794|5.0|Power is arguably the most influential five letter word in the English language.  Empires have been built and destroyed based upon the illusion of power alone.  Whether the power is factual or an illusion, the people wielding it have the ability to do great good and great evil.  In the book, A Game of Thrones, we can see how the word power affects people and to what extremes they will go to achieve their desired level of status.<br /> In the beginning of the book, we are introduced to four families:  the Starks, the Lannisters, the Baratheons, and the Targaryens.  Each of these represent a major player in the struggle for power through the story.  The Starks are the embodiment of what it means to be a family of nobility.  They are bound by honor, duty, and loyalty.  The Lannisters are a family driven by the lust and greed for power.  Their twisted sense of honor is dictated by their motto, \\"a Lannister always pays his debts.\\"  What is left of the Targaryens are the product of a dynasty driven mad by power.  The Baratheons seem to be the true losers in this tale, as their house is divided and destroyed as a result of attaining the crown.<br /> Eddard Stark is the lord of Winterfell and seems happy with his status.  That is until his friend and king, Robert Baratheon, asks him to be the hand of the king.  It is through his sense of duty, to not only the king but his friend, that he should leave his home and become the second most powerful man in the realm.  Due to his new found station Eddard discovers a truth about Robert's children that ultimately costs him his life.  Even though it is said \\"truth is power,\\" his honor forces him to make the grave mistake of warning his enemies to leave before he reveals the truth to Robert.<br /> Robert Baratheon is a warrior turned king who overthrew the Targaryen dynasty.  His rebellion is one based in vengeance from what he sees as a power-hungry and bloodthirsty family.  Once he is made king, he finds that his dealings in ruling a kingdom cause him to lose his character and the very essence of himself.  He even goes so far as marry Cersei Lannister for political means.<br /> The house of Lannister is a respected, feared, and hated house in the realm.  Their thirst for power has no bounds.  Their family can be found at various levels of power in the royal court and throughout the kingdom.  Their grasp of the realm has grown so bold that even the crown itself is indebted to them.<br /> The plot of this book follows multiple characters as the events unfold.  This allows us to understand the series of events from different points of view.  There are no defined protagonists or antagonists because each character forces you to empathize with them.  We can attribute this to the duality of man and perception itself.<br /> Though the story may be fantasy, the lessons learned in it hold a truth in every society.  The variety of characters presented represents all of humanity in deed and action.  The author seems to have done this to blur the lines of good and evil.  Inside of each of us there is a combination of Lannister, Stark, and Baratheon.  It is up to us which house we will embody during our own quest for power and success.a kingdom cause him to lose his character and the very essence of himself. 0671004107|5.0|Contact is one of those rarities where the book and the film of the book are of equal excellence.    The book works because the science is accurate, due to Carl Sagan being a physicist, astronomer and cosmologist and his underlying plot for the book... the interception of encoded transmissions from deep space being something that the scientific community was and is working on as embodied by SETI, the Search for Extra Terrestrial Intelligence, which was located at the Paracebo Telescope on the island of Puerto Rico.    The concept is a simple one. That a non Earth-based intelligence would probably try to communicate with other developed intelligences in the galaxy by the use of radio telecommunications. Sagan uses the intercepted broadcasts of radio pulses which occur in the same sequence as prime numbers and it is this property that identifies the signals as non-random radio transmissions which draws the attention of the astronomers and causes them to investigate further.    Eventually, the investigating astronomers discover a television signal underlying the pulses and when they reconstruct it they find themselves looking at a the broadcast of the 1936 Olympics in Berlin. The hypothesis is that the 1936 Olympic games in Berlin were the first TV transmissions which were broadcast around the world and that these had been recorded by another intelligence and re-broadcast to Earth. These TV pictures themselves had embedded within them the technical schematics for the building of a machine which would enable a human being to make a journey through space-time in order to gain knowledge of a more advanced civilization. I won't spoil the denouement.    Contact is a powerful book, because the detail is accurate and Sagan is stimulating our imaginations and yet staying within the realms of the actual and the 'doable'. The idea of traveling through a worm-hole, is, as yet, theoretical but according to Professor Stephen Hawkins, his theories do not break the Laws of Physics.Contact is a great work of science fiction and an uplifting one.[...]

B0070UFMOW|3.0|i bought this neat little gear to try out the external DAC/AMP scenery. i currently have a decent soundcard with Asahi AK4396 DAC / JRC 4580 Op Amp for analogue output. people always say external DAC/AMP will always be better than internal soundcard. so there i was, gave it a shot. i must say the result was underwhelming. i guess if you already have a decent soundcard that has a decent DAC and AMP, this \\"portable\\" DAC/AMP might not be better than what you already have. note the keyword \\"portable\\". you might have to go up to some serious professional, or high end external DAC/AMP in order to feel the quality difference. on paper, the e17's DAC (WM8740) actually is pretty close to AK4396. the WM8740 edges out slightly in THD, while the AK4396 edges out slightly in SNR and Dynamic Range.<br /><br />after all this is only a portable DAC/AMP so i guess one shouldn't expect too much out of it. i was just expecting it to crush my current soundcard setup, but it didn't. in fact, it did slightly worse than my current home setup.<br /><br />sound quality:<br />i did an extensive 36 hour A/B comparison between the e17 and my current Asahi AK4396 DAC / JRC 4580 Op Amp (Soundcard). it's clear that the e17 while does the mid range and treble really well, on par with the AK4396 and JRC4580, its low end response is lacking a whole lot. and i mean a LOT. i had to use the e17's internal eq and boost the bass up by 6db in order to get the same punch. i am not a person for eq, since you're just listening to electronics at that point, this immediately is a BIG negative for me in my book. equipment should sound their best when you get them, one shouldn't have to eq in order for it to sound good IMO. the e17 ever so slightly edges out on treble. but if you already have a very good set of cans, extra treble might actually be too much. i am using it with a Sennheiser HD598 at home. other than that, there really isn't much to write home about. bottom line, the e17 is ever so slightly on the bright side, while the AK/JRC combo is ever so slightly warmer. the difference in warmness and brightness between the two become VERY VERY slight once you bump the bass up by 6db on e17, that you probably will have to really look for it in order to tell the difference.<br /><br />this portable DAC/AMP works really well for a laptop setup, consider laptop's internal DAC/AMP is always poor. it's an upgrade for my 2010 MBP setup with the ATH AD700. and by bumping the bass up 6db, it brings it almost equal to what i have at home with the Asahi AK4396 DAC / JRC 4580 Op Amp. i say \\"almost\\" because the home setup doesn't require any eq at all.<br /><br />i'll probably keep the e17, since i can use it in the office, or when i am not home.<br /><br />if all you have is a dinky creative soundcard, or onboard, this will definitely be an upgrade. otherwise, if you already have a decent soundcard setup, it might not be an upgrade at all for you, in some B007TBZ63Y|5.0|Nobody ever got it right, until Rick Riordan. Not the comic books that made them superheroes, not the TV shows, and certainly not Disney. Who knew that Riordan could launch a best-selling YA series based on the absolute truth: all the gods are jerks. Complete SOBs.<br /><br />Riordan's Percy Jackson and Heroes of Olympus books give us that old-time religion. Percy and his friends are demi-gods-children with one mortal and one divine parent. Since the Greek/Roman gods procreate prolifically, but aren't big on parenting, their children start off with abandonment issues. Add to this that the monsters of mythology don't stay dead-they \\"respawn\\" after a few years or centuries-and there's nothing they like more than tasty young demi-gods, and survival becomes a real issue for the god's neglected offspring. The answer is for demi-gods, when they reach a certain age, to go gather in Camp Half-Blood to be trained as heroes (by Charon, centaur-trainer of greats like Jason and Hercules, no less).<br /><br />We of the modern world would notice all these demi-humans and monsters running around killing each other, if it wasn't for the Mist-a divinely imposed mass hallucination that makes us forget, dismiss, or explain anything \\"out of the ordinary.\\" No need for Men in Black to come flashy-thing us and tell us it was the light of Venus reflecting off of swamp gas or whatever. So we don't see the \\"terrorist attacks\\" and natural disasters around us for what they are-the fallout of hero-monster battles and harbingers of the End of The World.<br /><br />Because the world is, in fact, doomed but for the victories and sacrifices of our main characters, Percy (son of Neptune), Annabeth (daughter of Athena), and so on. And wonderful-and terrifying, and sometimes heartbreaking-adventures they are too. And here is where Riordan shines; he takes the old myths of the Greek gods, demi-gods, and monsters, and updates them for a modern story, but he doesn't pretty them up in any way.<br /><br />So here we see the Zeus who is an arbitrary ruler (and a serial seducer at best) and his homicidally jealous wife Hera, who loves making life miserable and short for her husband's many children (forget what Disney said-it wasn't Hades who tried to kill Hercules as an infant). The other gods aren't a whole lot better, some are worse. With absentee-parents like these it's a wonder their children survive at all, and don't think Riordan wasn't making a pointed social comment here.<br /><br />The stories are wonderful, and Percy, Annabeth, and their companions are fighting for the highest stakes; they must save mankind-first from domination by the Titans, then from extermination by Gaea and her other monstrous children. But as you read the stories, you realize why, whether it is Absolute Truth or merely another mythology, the Judeo-Christian worldview was a Good Idea that eventually supplanted the old-time pagan religions. Today we can read about these old-time gods but we can't understand them unless we look at them through the eyes of their demi-god children, who \\"worship\\" them-in the sense that they make offerings to them and follow orders-but don't love them. The old-time gods of the Hellenes (Greeks) demanded respect-but did not inspire adoration.<br /><br />Why not? Because the Greeks saw the world as arbitrary and unfair, and their gods reflected it; after all, the gods ruled the world, so they were responsible for it. Thus Ares was a bully-he personified military strength, nothing else, and to the victor go the spoils. Aphrodite and her son Eros personified love-which could wreck marriages, destroy alliances, inspire murder, etc. Zeus and Neptune personified the sky (weather) and the sea (also weather), the two forces that brought the Greeks-farmers and sea-going merchants-prosperity or tragedy. If the gods smiled on you, you prospered, if they frowned, you failed, so you'd better worship them with offerings and rituals if you knew what was good for you. But evenif you did all that, and kept the few moral laws (mostly civic laws) that they considered important, there was no guarantee of the god's favor; fate was fickle, leading Solon, one of Athens' greatest historical figures to declare \\"Call no man happy who is not dead.\\"<br /><br />So for Percy and the gang, happiness is not guaranteed even if they win. The gods don't have their best interests at heart, and often obstruct their quests. Each book contains the potential for tragedy (heroes die), and therefor for great heroism as they struggle against sometimes hopeless odds. You know that they will win in the end, but you cringe at the cost.<br /><br />The Percy Jackson and The Heroes of Olympus books are written for the Young Adult market, and certainly Riordan gains strength as a storyteller as the series progresses, but I cannot recommend these books strongly enough.if you did all that, and kept the few moral laws (mostly civic laws) that they considered important, there was no guarantee of the god's favor; fate was fickle, leading Solon, one of Athens' greatest historical figures to declare \\"Call no man happy who is not dead.\\"<br /><br />So for Percy and the gang, happiness is not guaranteed even if they win. The gods don't have their best interests at heart, and often obstruct their quests. Each book contains the potential for tragedy (heroes die), and therefor for great heroism as they struggle against sometimes hopeless odds. B0083SBMBM|5.0|I picked up a copy of The Avengers on Bluray last Tuesday afternoon intent on watching it that evening even though I wasn't feeling well. I have never used an inhaler. Went home and took the antibiotics at 6pm, then the inhaler at 7pm. Sat down to watch the Avengers with the kids at 7pm. I had no side effects from the inhaler at that time.<br /><br />The Avengers was wickedly funny and I ended up laughing and then coughing a lot. I had a really bad coughing fit at the point in the movie where Loki starts monologueing at the Hulk and the Hulk grabs him and beats the ground with him like he was a rag doll. Great stuff.<br /><br />Then around 9pm I noticed a strange white washing effect in my left eye. I went into the kitchen to see if it was just from the dark room with the TV. The effect started to get worse and I called my wife in. I felt almost like I was going to pass out so I sat down on the floor. I started to sweat. My first reaction was that I was having a reaction to the inhaler. The white washing was getting worse. My wife was going to take me to urgent care, I tried to move across the floor on my hands and knees, got 5 feet and then sat back against the wall and told her to call 911. It felt like gravity had quadrupled.<br /><br />The ambulance arrived at 9:24. My left eye was still goofed up, getting worse. Right eye was 100% fine. I was still sweating, and was speaking with a slow, measured gate. Some initial assessments included holding my two arms out. In doing so, my right hand was dropping. These were signs of a stroke. I did not have any facial drooping. I was able to crawl out the front door to make it easier to get onto the stretcher.<br /><br />They took me to the Hospital in Grafton, a 30 minute ride. St. Joe's was closer, but I would only take someone there if I knew they were certain to die. By the midpoint of the ride, I could no longer lift my right hand and had a pretty good idea I was having a stroke. Alas, it does not seem that was meant to be. By the time we got to the hospital in Grafton, I had regained full use and strength of my right hand and my speech was back to normal. My left eye was still goofed up, with everything being all white-washy and sparkly like.<br /><br />Into the hospital, they did some assessments, ran a CT and X-ray and started an IV. Everything seemed better except for the eye, but that was improving. They had gotten hold of a neurologist and he ordered up a CT with contrast. During the scan, when the injector shot the contrast into my arm, the needle backed out of the vein and ended up filling my arm instead. It hurt like a SOB. Given the speed at which the technologist was able to figure that something had gone very wrong (sloth speed), I declined her offer to try again in my other arm, opting instead to wait until morning. I went back to the emergency waiting area (still on my stretcher) where my wife was waiting. It was just after midnight at this point, marking the official start of our 20th wedding anniversary. They put me up in a room for the night, freaked out of my mind.<br /><br />I got the contrast CT in the morning (Wednesday)around 11am and although it was of poor quality, it did show blockage in both my right and left carotids. I was still having some of the visual effects in my left eye. The initial prognosis was dissected carotids on both the left and right sides.  The doctors started me on heprin. I took a nap and when I woke around 1:40pm the eye issue had gone away.  Then decided to send me to St. Luke's as it is the premier hospital in Milwaukee for strokes and such. I arrived late afternoon (very nice ambulance ride) and was delivered to the Neuro ICU. I met with several doctors and the plan was to perform an angiogram in the AM on Thursday. The staff was friendly and courteous.<br /><br />Thursday morning they sent me for an MRI first and then for the Angio. The MRI was at 9am. There were some really cool protocols used, one of them sounded like sound effects from the old donkey kong game. The Angiogram went smooth without a hitch. It was a very nice Siemens scanner with a panel of displays that would make any NASA staffer drool.  Afterward, the doctor came by with my news. The MRI showed that I did not actually have a stroke (no dain bramage) but based on my symptoms, came ridiculously close. The Angio showed about 90% blockage on the left side and 50% on the right. It also showed that the blood flow was straight and smooth. They kept me in ICU for another day for observation.<br /><br />The course of treatment is now rat poison. I was transferred to a regular patient room, out of the ICU, on Friday evening. They started the warfarin at that time. The last few days have then been laying around waiting for results on the warfarin levels. For those that have not been through it, they give you the pill at night, then sneak in and steal some blood around 4am and let you know around 8am if you have to stay another day. I got the clearance to leave Tuesday morning at 11am (I ended up just a few hours shy of a full weeks stay). I will now be on the rat poison for the next month at which time I go back for a follow up scan. Depending on what they see, I could keep taking the rat poison or be able to stop it.<br /><br />The doctors indicated we will likely never know what caused this. Typically it is caused by blunt force trauma to the neck. There was nothing like that. The only thing I could figure is that it was from the forceful coughing I was doing.<br /><br />So, bottom lining it, based on the fact that it almost gave me a stroke and blew out both my carotids, I have no choice but to give The Avengers 5 stars.

You will need to adjust your chair a certain way, generally looking straight forward at the unit gives you the only working angle, where it doesnt end up too dark. PS ONE games can be unplayable unless you use the highest brightness and contrasst, the settings on this thing are really wacky and the screen itself (other than it's hd imagery) is low quality and they definately cut corners. There are ghosting issues and the screens very thin, pressingon at all it WILL cause physical pressure damage. It seems to hold everything sturdy and work well for gaming, scratches are either all over the screen boarder and unit or at a minimum its a gamble when you buy it. my first defective unit had scracthes all over the screen boarder, this one has none. All in all it works for portable gaming if thats all you want and are willing to go through multiple units if it does have water damage (it sounds like most do) good luck as it seems to be a crap shoot. If you are using this as your main gaming machine and are bothered by craftmanship defects such as screen bubbles, scratches, and difficult viewing angles, you will want to buy a regular TV. Since this product is fairly cheap i'll recommend it to those looking for a cheap way to play games while traveling and that's it. its a one star for anyone else, because of defects, poor lighting and viewing angles, and general cutting corners to keep the price low If they worked on making a better product and upped the price it would be better, but they didnt want to charge higher prices, so that's how it is unfortunately.<br /><br />having to keep a unit because i dont feel like jumping hoops that has minor water damage lining the screen, and fumbling with bad viewing angles isn't what i should have to do at all, so i cant recommend this product to most people... but as i said it does do what it is suppose to do other than that. its a 720p hdtv in a suitcase.<br /><br />I also wish they had a number for tech support, email only is bad, although they do respond and are helpful. I feel very limited here, so whatever they tell you in email, goes. your best way to get your money back is entirely through amazon so their tech support most likely wont be needed.

B005LW5J9O|5.0|This is a fascinating look at the influence of classical cultural documents on the early renaissance. It focuses on the scholarly research of Poggio Braccolini,whose love of classical literature led him to a discovery of one of the most important poems of the ancient era, On the Nature Of Things by Lucretius.He and others of his time faced profound internal struggles between the values of classical humanism they were reading in these texts and their Catholicism. They were influenced by the approaches of Epicurus and Lucretius, men of the ancient world, they had discovered, who accepted atheism and who had a world view that was remarkable as a precursor to a  modern scientific ethos.<br />These men, including Poggio, who rose from the ranks to become the Pope's secretary were often in the Papal inner circle, where such philosophies were extremely dangerous. Swerve is an insider's expose of the political and social intrigues of the Papal environment, which  makes present day corruption seem sanitized and tame. In addition, it ties the intrigues of the early renaissance Papacy to how these ideas of the ancients survived suppression to influence the emergence of the modern world because of the scholasticism and reverence of these men.  [[ASIN:0393343405 The Swerve: How the World Became Modern]]

0316228532|4.0|Imagine for a moment that you are J.K. Rowling. Your internationally bestselling novels about an orphan boy who attends an imaginary school for wizards and witches, over which you labored for 10 years, have sold hundreds of millions of copies. They have been translated into 73 languages and turned into eight blockbuster movies. Harry Potter is now and probably will remain a household name for decades. You are reportedly the richest woman in England (even richer than your queen), quite possibly the world. You are A-listed for every conceivable event on the planet. Would you rest on your laurels, perhaps buy a private jet and a yacht or two, and tour the world?<br /><br />Suppose instead that your life consists of an occasional commencement address and the rare television interview, but you would rather retain your privacy and work with disadvantaged children that harken back to your own humble beginnings. And your creative mind continues to nudge you that you still have stories to tell.<br /><br />This is how Rowling's eagerly awaited new novel, THE CASUAL VACANCY, came about. By virtue of her fame, over one million copies were pre-sold before the book hit the stores. Readers expecting the charm and magic of Harry Potter are in for a surprise. This serious, often dark and sometimes humorous novel is a tale of the inhabitants of the small, west England village of Pagford, similar to the one where Rowling grew up. She has brought them to life through a half-dozen middle-class families who share career, political and social ambitions. Their children attend the same schools; several are teenagers who live in their own worlds of cell phones, laptops and social networking. Between Pagford and a nearby medium-sized city that provides industry and commerce to the region lies a crumbling housing project known as The Fields. The Fields are rife with the problems attendant to poverty --- drugs, prostitution, alcoholism, unemployment, hopelessness --- and an especially poignantfamily, the Weedons.<br /><br />Pagford's governing council is led by its most popular and beloved councilman, Barry Fairbrother. Barry's unexpected death at age 42 brings about a rift in the community as selection begins to elect his replacement. The council was about to vote on the continuation of a controversial methadone clinic in The Fields, a project that Barry had strongly supported. This event becomes a tragic catalyst for destruction. Relationships between married couples, parents and their teenage children, and long-term friendships become frayed as the ghost of Barry Fairbrother seems to hover over the conscience of the village.<br /><br />There are no magic potions or wands in Pagford to mitigate the problems that lay just below the surface of the idyllic little village. The all-too-human inhabitants of Pagford are left to fend for themselves, as must we all. Rowling has turned her storytelling mastery to a subject that has made the book controversial in England. It is a story of the clash between classes and is very British in its view of the haves versus the have-nots. The humor, dialect and vocabulary are often colloquial, so if you are unfamiliar with British slang and phrases, you may be nonplussed on occasion. For instance, if one is not familiar with the difference between \\"public\\" and \\"private\\" schools in the UK --- the exact opposite of the American education system --- it can be confusing. Also, reference to \\"living on the estate\\" in England means that you live in publicly financed housing. Going home to the estate is not driving through the gates to your mansion containing rare works of art, a pool and tennis courts in Beverly Hills. It is far more likely to be a tenement whose art is defined by graffiti.<br /><br />Class consciousness is a popular theme of novels, even in America's coveted ideal of democracy. THE STARBOARD SEA, the acclaimed first novel by Amber McDermont, takes place in a similar American eastern small town. It is a story ofclass warfare between the privileged students of an exclusive boarding school and the \\"townies.\\" Much like CATCHER IN THE RYE of an earlier era, these and books like them define the ever-present pecking order of the human condition. Will A CASUAL VACANCY by an author of the stature of J.K. Rowling compare? Only time will tell.<br /><br />Reviewed by Roz Shea

I don't rate it as high as season one for the simple fact that it is true that there are several episodes that are just plain boring.  It's still worth a watching if you are a fan of the show or just simply like zombie flicks.
055380684X|3.0|The Talent Code, by Daniel Coyle, is a book that explores how talent is developed.  After reading the literature, querying experts in academic towers, talking to master coaches, and pounding the pavement to places where talent unexpectedly emerges, Coyle concludes as his subtitle purports that, \\"Greatness isn't born.  It's grown.\\"  Coyle definitely takes you along for an incredible ride on this elegantly written journey.<br /><br />Coyle takes a fascinating look into the psychology of learning and expertise and offers a provocative look at the world  of talent.  All this is well worth the read and is incredible food for thought.  Unfortunately, there is a major flaw here: Coyle isn't interested in other theories of learning or data that doesn't support his thesis. Still, although I do not agree completely with Coyle's outlook, I have to thank him for an excellent read and getting the gears in my head turning.<br /><br />I almost gave this one 4 stars: I found the book infectiously readable and it inspired me to think about and look into research on a very interesting topic.  However, I believe that there are some severe problems with Coyle's conclusions that I will address later in the review.  On rereading my review, these flaws caused me to downgrade my rating to 3 stars. Please realize that in the limited context of an Amazon review, I cannot address all the problems brought up in a 256-page book. Therefore, I have chosen to critique topics that I felt were pivotal and the ones that interested me the most.<br /><br />THE MAJOR POINTS OF THE BOOK/COYLE's POINT OF VIEW<br />As noted above, Coyle's main thesis is that talent and expertise are developed by training and do not rely on innate abilities or genetics. The first part of the book is devoted to discussing how deep practice results in talent and the physiological basis of this.<br /><br />Deliberate (Deep) Practice:<br />His proof is based heavily on deliberate practice theory as demonstrated by K.Anders Ericsson and others.  The deliberate practice school of thought asserts  that genetics have little or nothing to do with the development of talent.  Instead talent (and even genius) is developed by highly targeted practice (called deep practice or deliberate practice), where the learner maintains a laser-like focus on the activity or subject at hand and responds promptly to critical feedback.<br /><br />In deep practice the mind doesn't wander, it hones in on the desired outcome paying close attention to recognizing errors and correcting them.  There may be different methods of achieving deep practice, but the goal of learner is to work at the 'sweet spot'--at the razors edge of his or her ability.  As the theory goes it takes about 10 years/10,000 hours of deep practice to become an expert in any field.<br /><br />The theory is based on seminal work by Ericsson and others, who have observed deep practice at work in the laboratory, calculated the hours logged by experts in many fields prior to achieving expert status, and even analyzed the lives of historical figures.  In all cases, these researhers have found that the 10 year/10,000 hour rule applies.<br /><br />As Coyle would tell us, the moral of the story is that experts, geniuses, savants etc. are NOT somehow special, they have exactly the same machinery as the rest of us.  Coyle boils it down to a simple equation: deep practice x 10,000 hours= world-class skill.  The conclusion is two fold: 1) deliberate practice is what drives learning, and; 2)innate/genetic factors do not matter in the development of expertise, even genius. If you put in your 10 years/10,000 hours you're as good as gold.<br /><br />Coyle backs all this up with scientific studies and some pointed observations that show deep practice at work  He also visits so-called Chicken-wire Harvards--small, humble, and unlikely places that produce mammoth amounts of talent.  Coyle also shows how talent is fostered through expert teaching and coaching.  All this, he quite convincingly exhorts, is the recipe for creating expert talent and genius.<br /><br />Coyle also takes a foray into the physiological basis of learning.  Drawing on the work of Douglas Fields and George Bartzokis, he shows how the developing myelin is the key to developing learned skills.  Basically, myelin is the substance that wraps around neuron (nerve cell) axons (the long projections that jut out from nerve cells that propagate nerve signals).  Myelin insulates, speeds up, and fine tunes nerve signaling.  Because \\"every human movement, thought, or feeling is a precisely timed electric signal traveling down chains of neurons\\" having maximally aligned, networked, and efficient neurons is the key to learning.  For Coyle learning=the development of myelin.<br /><br />Once Coyle has discussed how deliberate (deep) practice is the key to developing talent and expertise, he uses the second part of the book to show concrete examples of how deep practice is motivated. In the third part of the book Coyle show how master coaches (knowingly and unknowingly) use deep practice to train elite athletes and musicians.<br /><br />The epilogue takes a very brief (so brief it was unsatisfying to this reader) look at how the principles outlined in the book might apply to education, business, psychology, aging, and parenting.<br /><br />CRITICISMS, CONTROVERSIES, AND CONUNDRUMS:<br /><br />Make no mistake, all of the above makes for some incredibly interesting and engaging reading.  However, as I read I started to ask some important questions and started searching outside sources for answers.  Please realize that I am no psychologist or neuroscientist (and I sure as heck have not put in the requisite 10,000 hours to become an expert in these areas).  However, even my superficial reading of the literature brought up some controversies that Coyle simply avoids.<br /><br />My goal here is NOT to pop Coyle's bubble or to suggest that he is some kind of charlatan.  He weaves an interesting story and a story that is supported by some eminent experts in the field.  However, Coyle does not present a balanced picture as he leads you down the garden path.  Some critical information is often left out and some of his explanations--although seemingly internally consistent and downright captivating--are more controversial than he lets on and are easily subject to alternative interpretations.<br /><br />As noted above, there is not enough room to fully address all the issues that are brought up by the book. I will try to address the ones that I felt were more pivotal and more interesting to me personally.<br /><br />DIFFICULT DEFINITIONS<br />Talent, expertise, and genius are difficult terms to begin with.  For example, we all agree that proficiency in, say, a musical instrument such as the violin requires talent, but it is often difficult to define what 'true talent' is.  For some, it may mean that someone is able to play a tune by ear.  For others, it may mean that you can play the violin better than most people who are able to play the instrument.  Still to others, it may mean that the talented person must be able to play some of the most complex music ever written for the violin or become a coveted soloist.<br /><br />Expertise and genius are also difficult to define.  How do you define levels of expertise?  When do you know that you have reached it?  Is genius simply being among the best in your field? Does this imply that that you need only a certain level of technical expertise or does it imply that you have to have the ability to create something groundbreaking and new?  Is being an elite athlete simply being able to play better than most people?  Does it mean that you have to be able to play your sport on a professional or Olympic level? What if you play an off-beat sport or come from a country that has lower overall athletic standards? Does it mean that your in the top 30, 20, or 10?  For students in the KIPP schools, success is goingto college.  But for someone who is interested in winning the Nobel Prize, college is but a stepping stone.<br /><br />This ambiguity often makes it difficult to judge some of the claims posited in the book. If you can't agree on what level defines genius or expertise, it's hard to judge whether the 10/10,000 rule applies.  If it's too 'easy' to reach a certain goal, it will, on average, take less than 10/10,000 to get there.  If the definition of 'elite' is too limited (say making it to the podium in the Olympics) and 10/10,000 may not be enough to achieve it (e.g. only three athletes can win a medal in a given sport no matter how much practice the top athletes have put in).<br /><br />Coyle isn't all that helpful in this respect.  He defines talent in a footnote as, \\"the possession of repeatable skills that don't depend on physical size\\" but doesn't clarify how to define genius or expertise.  Which only leaves us with a Justice-Potter-Stewartistic concept of \\"But I know it when I see it...\\"<br /><br />DELIBERATE PRACTICE THEORY/NATURE VERSUS NURTURE:<br />For Coyle and those who adhere to 'orthodox' deliberate practice theory, almost every aspect of talent is determined by deep practice.  This is fine, but it ignores other theories and studies that have real data that could potentially poke holes in the deep practice x 10,000 hours= world-class skill equation.<br /><br />The argument seems to boil down to two distinct issues:<br />1) Whether deliberate (deep) practice improves performance<br />2) Whether deliberate practice (10 year/10,000 hours) is an equalizer without regard to genetic differences in cognitive/athletic abilities (with the caveat that the subjects cannot have organic or acquired cognitive deficits and that some innate physical characteristics, such as height and weight, may make some better or lesser suited for certain athletic pursuits).<br /><br />As to #1, there is little argument. No one contests that deliberate practice will result in improvement of performance.  Even scientists who feel that there is a so-called 'innate' component to talent do not believe that babies are experts upon exiting the womb.  The 'innate' component would simply represent a potential in a given area.  Without deliberate practice, the innate component is irrelevant.<br /><br />The dicey part is conjecture #2.  Is the 10 year/10,000 hours of deliberate practice enough to make you an expert or are there differences between subjects that can't be explained by deliberate practice alone?  Coyle presents the 10/10,000 theory as accepted without question, but a quick look at the scientific literature shows that a healthy debate is raging.<br /><br />There are studies such as those of Baker et al., (JASP, 15: 12--25, 2003), where expertise for certain sports was found to be less than 10,000 hours.  Additionally, Baker et al showed that there was variation between subjects in terms of the amount of deep practice required to become an expert (Basketball 5772-6026 hours, Field Hockey 864-5164 hours, Netball 600-3440 hours). Campitelli and Gobet looked at the role of practice in chess (Developmental Psychology, 43, 159-172) and found that the average amount of deliberate practice necessary for reaching master level was considerable 11,053 hours (very close to the golden 10,000 hours). However, there was also a substantial amount of variability between subjects, with the slowest player requiring 23,608 hours and the fastest player 3,016 hours. Some players with more than 25,000 hours of deliberate practice never reached the master level. [These are only examples.  There are many similar studies done by these and other authors.]<br /><br />You can decide to read, for example, the Campitelli/Gobet paper in any number of ways, including: 1) There is an error in the data collection (lack of understanding about what deliberate practice is/under or over reporting of time by subjects); 2) There is a flaw in the study design; 3) Some subjects performed similar intellectual or physical tasks that were NOT chess based that either helped with chess performance or confounded their ability to perform well at chess; 4) there are other differences (innate, environmental, etc.) between subjects that explain why the 10/10,000 rule doesn't seem to apply.<br /><br />Any way you slice it, however, the data as it stands is not consistent with the 10/10,000 hypothesis.  Please realize that this is not my opinion: it is the data that does not fit with the 10/10,000 hypothesis.  Either the data is in error or some other explanation needs to be found. Please remember, these scientists think statistically.  There isn't any disagreement that there are clear differences between groups (expert, novice, etc.).  There are two bones of contention: 1) do all activities require the same 10/10,000 hours, and; 2) do all subjects require the same amount of time to reach expertise (are the variations between the subjects within each groups statistically significant enough to show that some people require more or less than 10/10,000).<br /><br />My opinion as a non-expert in the field, for whatever it is worth, is that the 10/10,000 hypothesis is a little naive.  All biological systems exhibit variability.  It would be hard to believe that mental and physical capabilities--two incredibly important capabilities that work hand-in-hand when it comes to natural selection/survival--would not exhibit subject-to-subject variability.<br /><br />Anecdotal, non-scientific observations at least hint that not all experts are the same.  When it comes to elite athletes, there are those who consistently fall within the top ten and even fewer who consistently fall within the top three at the World Games and the Olympics.  There are chess masters who consistently win international tournaments and those who don't. The key here is that it is very hard for me to believe that elite athletes who consistently place within the top ten but never quite make the podium are somehow more deficient in deliberate practice when compared to the medalists.  It is hard for me to believe that the chess masters who fall within the top ten or twenty but don't consistently win international tournaments aren't somehow trying hard enough.<br /><br />We also often see that, when working with a group, there are people who simply pick things up more quickly than others.  As Dean Keith Simonton--a researcher that puts a 20 to 30% premium on innate abilities--might put it, those with the golden genes might get more 'bang for the buck' when practicing.  (20 to 30% seems quite high to me, but at the extremes of performance, even a 1 to 2% innate difference might make a difference.)<br /><br />Note as well that there is a very big difference between being highly proficient or even an expert at something and making that leap to genius, where someone makes creative connections and comes up with something completely new. Deliberate practice theorists feel that these creative connections are simply the end result of enough deliberate practice.  But this doesn't explain, for example, why only Liebniz and Newton were the only ones of their generation who came up with the idea of calculus and why only Newton was able to write the Principia where he was able to use calculus to explain natural phenomena. After all, there were other mathematical experts at the time that had all the same tools at hand. Moreover, these tools were available for many, many years prior to the invention of calculus.<br /><br />Before you accuse me of determinism and cynicism, please realize that I am NOT saying that the 10/10,000 theory is wrong. I am stating that it has not been proved beyond a shadow of a doubt. There is data that needs to be explained, so the jury is still out.<br /><br />Science may not care about optimism, but I do. So the simple answer here is that, at very least, deliberate practice theory has demonstrated that we are all far more capable than we may think. There is incredible plasticity within our brains and bodies such that we CAN leverage our physiology with deliberate practice. It may take some 23,608 hours to become a chess master and others 3,016 hours, but the end result is still the same. Even in the worst case scenario where someone spends many, many hours but never becomes a master, his or her performance is still VASTLY improved over his/her baseline with deliberate practice.<br /><br />So, even at very worst, there is no futility here, just a more measured look at the outcomes. Even if the 10/10,000 theory needs to be qualified, there is no way to measure the extent of my capabilities prior to practicing and it doesn't change the fact that I can vastly improve my performance through deliberate practice.<br /><br />PRODIGIES<br />Coyle discounts the existence of prodigies: prodigies are simply the product of driven behavior and unusual amounts of deliberate practice at a much younger age than average.  He points to studies that show that  although prodigies might shine at a young age, on deeper analysis they do not exhibit true expertise/genius until they reach the 10/10,000 hour limit.  Moreover, he cites studies that show that prodigies are overrated in that they are not any more likely to be successful in the long run than persons who start their 10/10,000 hours at a later age.<br /><br />Reviewing the literature, the prodigy studies are historical in nature and therefore 'fuzzy' science for me.  On the one hand it is convincing to know that Mozart likely had 3,500 hours of music instruction from his father by age 6 and that he didn't achieve his genius in composition until his 20s.  (Of course, he did transcribe Allegri's Miserere note for note from memory after only hearing it twice and composed a successful opera around age 14...  However, a lot of historical claims for prodigies were fabricated or their age was fudged to enhance the myth.)  Some prodigy pundits also point out that being a prodigy is not a good predictor of long term success.<br /><br />Still, other researchers point to the existence of modern prodigies whose lives have been well documented.  For example, Sarah Chang, a successful violinist who received a violin by her 4th birthday, was accepted in Julliard at age 5, and who was a soloist with the New York Philharmonic at age 8.  This kind of history gives one pause.  As to lack of success for prodigies, it could be argued that success is generally a difficult thing for anyone to achieve.  Moreover, we are plagued by the absence of a denominator.  It is hard to say that prodigies are any more or less successful than the rest of society when we can't calculate a true success rate for either group.<br /><br />(Other possibilities: prodigies may 'burn out' after spending excessive time early on in a singular pursuit.  Alternatively, you could apply a somewhat circular style of reasoning and state that prodigies who don't make it were not 'true' prodigies at all.  They were simply people with average capabilities that applied the 10/10,000 rule and looked somehow remarkable because they reached their peak earlier than most.)<br /><br />CHICKEN-WIRE HARVARDS<br />Coyle points to Chicken-wire Harvards--humble hotbeds of talent production that have few major resources but create massive amounts of talent.  I won't mention all the places that Coyle does, but will point out some discrepancies.  Coyle paints a deliberate picture of these chicken-wire Harvards.  Often, however, he leaves off important information that might allow a place to be seen in a different light.  He also offers a single, plausible interpretation of events that fits with his thesis, neglecting other plausible interpretations that don't quite fit his theory.<br /><br />--Meadowmount School of Music: a remote music camp in Upstate New York that is well known to produce major talent.  First, Meadowmount is really a 7-week summer program, not a full blown music school. There is an auditioning process.  You don't make it into Meadowmount unless you are talented to begin with.  Let's look at some graduates by doing a quick web search... Pinchas Zuckerman: began music studies at age 4; started violin at age 8; discovered at age 14 and shipped off to Julliard.  So, he did spend time at Meadowmount, but he also received extensive training in Israel and at Julliard.  Yo-Yo Ma: began violin and viola, until he settled on the cello at age 4. Performed concerts at age 5.  Performed concerts for US Presidents at age 7.  Television debut at age 8.  Studied at Julliard and eventually enrolled in the not-so-chicken-wire Harvard University.  That said, I don't want to take anything away from Meadowmount.  It may be that the school left an indelible impression on Zuckerman and Ma.  But, however seminal an experience, it is hard to give all the credit to Meadowmount for these and other already extremely talented musicians who crossed the Meadowmount threshold.<br /><br />--The Spartak Tennis Club: a \\"freezing junkpile that has produced a volcano of talent.\\"  Of the students mentioned, I found some quick information on Anna Kournikova, Marat Safin, and Dinara Safin.  Kournikova attended Spartak from age 6.  By age 10, she was shipped off to Brandenton, Florida where she trained in a much more posh setting with Nick Bolletieri.  Marat Safin and Dinara Safina's parents were on staff at Spartak.  Marat trained there until age 14 and Dinara until age 8, when both moved to Spain and more stylish quarters.  It took Marat 3 years and Dinara 6 years in Spain before going pro.  Spartak starts off with many, many young tennis players and ends up promoting the few that make the grade.  There is no question that Spartak is definitely a place where raw talent can be found, but spotting raw talent is a somewhat different skill than nurturing that talent to fruition. (That said, the staff at Spartak will have to duke it out with Bolletieri, regarding who had the most influenceon Kournikova's career.)<br /><br />--Futsal:  Futsal is a quick paced, soccer-like game that is played on a much smaller field.  The game developed in Brazil and Coyle attributes the development of Futsal to the success of Brazilian soccer. He attributes this to the fact that Futsal is a much faster game than soccer, players get more action, and that players touch the ball more times than in regular soccer.  I believe that all this has a certain historical validity. But as Coyle also points out, Brazil has an incredible, national interest in soccer but its cities have a paucity of open fields in which to play soccer.  By shrinking the game down in size, futsal courts can be found all over Brazilian cities.  This makes futsal incredibly available to the people in the same way basketball is brought to the people in the United States.  Interest+available courts=more people playing the game.  More people playing the game gives you a larger pool to pull from in terms of talent and a place to hone it.  That said, there's evidence here for the effects of deliberate practice, but it doesn't rule out the ability to winnow out 'natural' talent, if such a thing exists.<br /><br />OH, MY! OH, MYELIN!<br />Coyle goes so far as to produce a physiological correlate to deliberate practice. I do understand that the science of how we learn on a cellular level is of interest to Coyle.  At first, I thought that the meylin stuff was mostly used as an easy-to-explain metaphor for how learning occurs in the central nervous system.  In fact, I wouldn't even comment on this topic at all if it wasn't such a pervasive feature of the book.  By the end, however, I was almost banging my head against the wall when Coyle mentioned myelin... over and over and over... and over.<br /><br />So here goes: Generally, we are not born with nerve cells within our brains that are maximally aligned, maximally networked, or maximally efficient.  The nerve cells are basically the working units of the brain and communicate with each other by transferring electrical signals.  These signals are transported by projections off the nerve cell called axons. By improving the reliability, speed, and accuracy of nerve signals, we improve our skills. (I'm okay with this, so far.)<br /><br />Coyle's assertion is that by performing deep practice, we improve our myelin, allowing for maximally efficient nerve circuits.  For Coyle, the development of talent IS the development of myelin.<br /><br />I'm not against scientific revelations. I realize that the glial cells that produce the myelin were much maligned when I was learning this stuff in high school biology.  Nerve cells themselves were the stars of the show and the myelin producing glial cells were just `helper cells' that kept those sexy neurons happy.  Yes.  Glial cells and myelin are far more important than we ever thought before.<br /><br />Once again, I'm no neuroscientist, but a quick canvass of the literature reveals that learning is NOT simply the production of myelin.  It is a far more complex process.  Learning=myelin is a convenient metaphor but it is not the whole ball of wax.  To give a really poor analogy, the learning=myelin metaphor is like saying that to get full function from an internal combustion engine, firing six cylinders is better than firing only four cylinders.  Yeah, that's true, but if the spark plugs aren't in place, none of the cylinders will function.  And if there's no crankshaft, your wheels will never turn.  And by playing around with a bunch of gear ratios and improving efficiency, you can probably squeeze out a lot more function with one four cylinder set up than another.<br /><br />If you talk to a myelin scientist, my guess is that they get a little over excited and start stating theory as a fact.  So I'm going to go out on a limb here and throw a wrench into some of Coyle's stranger assertions:<br /><br />1) Maybe we have 20% more myelin than monkeys do, but this isn't the only reason why monkeys can't speak.  At least one of the other reasons has to do with well developed language centers that are genetically hardwired into our brains.  You could myelinate the crap out of a monkey and it still wouldn't have human speech capabilities.  (Then again, monkeys don't have to put up with bad reality TV, so they may have an evolutionary advantage here.)<br /><br />2) I can't find a paper to support evidence of increased myelin in the brains breast fed babies as opposed to formula fed ones.  But even assuming that breast fed babies get more Omega 3 fatty acids than their formula fed counterparts and that this actually does give them more myelin, there may be psychosocial reasons why they are smarter; for example they may have a closer bond with their mother.  See Harlow's monkey experiments for an extreme example of how nurturing might affect fear, security, and attachment in a baby, all of which potentially could affect intelligence. Myelin may play a part here, but it's hard to saythat myelin is the only issue.<br /><br />3) Michael Jordan's musculoskeletal system is just as subject to age as his neural network.  I'm sure his myelin was affected by age, but age related declines in athletic performance likely reflect age-related changes in all body systems.<br /><br />4) Neanderthals may have supplanted Cro-Magnon man, but it would be hard to pin it all on myelin.  First, there are other components apart from myelin that are responsible for intellectual function.  Moreover, there might have been other genetic and environmental factors involved.<br /><br />5) Horses walk at birth, as do many large mammals.  It's hard for me to believe that this is simply a myelin factor.  A human infant (or rat infant or mouse infant) may have a myelin deficiency, but they also lack a musculoskeletal system that can support their weight.  Horses need to walk at birth for survival, so all this is built in.  Myelin may be a part of the story, but it's not the entire story.<br /><br />6) Adolescents may make bad decisions, but it's certainly not only because their frontal lobe isn't myelinated enough.  Myelination does increase as adolescents grow older, but this is correlational data.  It would be impossible to say whether frontal lobe myelination is simply an age related change that has nothing to do with decisions making capacity or something that truly affects higher cognition and decision making.  For example, adolescents don't make better decisions because they go through growth spurts and become taller. Let's not forget as well that adolescence is accompanied with many, many other changes that could affect decision making; for example, hormonal changes.<br /><br />CONCLUSION:<br />Coyle has written a very interesting and very provocative book.  My main gripe is that the book is one sided--Coyle did not present a balanced view of the state of the science of learning.  A more nuanced view would likely have been more difficult to write, but would probably have been even more interesting to read.  Still, The Talent Code brings up extremely profound and compelling issues regarding development of expertise and compelled me to look more deeply at these issues.  It may not be perfect, but for me it was well worth the read.

B007V2UGPE|4.0|I did not have high expectations for this game when I downloaded it.  The title alone had my thinking that it was going to be some kind of crappy Plants vs. Zombies rip-off.  But I couldn't argue with the price, so I gave it a download.<br /><br />And as it turns out, I was pleasantly surprised.  It plays somewhat like a single-played MOBA game (LoL, HoN, Dota 2) but mixed with an RTS.  You have a hero, a samurai initially, though later you can unlock a zombie hero to play as.  This hero has to fight off waves of enemies on a side-scrolling field and keep them from destroying his base.  You can move the hero by tapping and holding either side of the screen and he will automatically use his basic attacks when in range of his opponent.  The skill element of the game comes in knowing when to move him in to attack and when to keep him away from the various enemies that he fights.  His initial melee attack can be used to knock enemies back and slow them down, this is very useful for buying time for the other aspect of the game, cooldowns and units.<br /><br />As the game progresses, you build up leadership points which allow you to deploy units to fight the zombies, such as farmers, warriors, archers, heroes, and other troops.  You are also allowed to choose from two abilities that you bring with you in to the fight, which confer bonuses such as, summoning additional temporary troops, performing an AoE attack on enemies, or slowing the enemy army for a short amount of time.<br /><br />Enemies drop currency and items when can be used in between levels to upgrade your hero, his weapons, his units, or to buy and upgrade structures that aid in base defense, such as a bell that damages demons, or archers that shoot enemies that get too close to the base.<br /><br />There is a surprising amount of depth and effort that went into this game.  The music is fitting, and the sound effects are great.  The units are all very well done and are easily distinguishable in the field of battle, the animations are smooth, and I only encounter the very smallest of framerate issues at times.  Really the only things that bug me at times are the inevitable occurrence in pretty much any game you get for 'Free' these days.<br /><br />As I mentioned before you buy upgrades in between waves.  But it's worth mentioning that some of these upgrades cannot be purchased with the normal currency that you get for beating levels.  These upgrades can only be bought with special coins, which only ever drop VERY rarely in the game, or purchased from the game makers, for real money.  Now, it's worth mentioning that these upgrades are far from essential for playing the game, I've gotten 30 or so levels in with no need to spend even a single cent, the drops from enemies and from the pachinko mini-game more than enough to see me through up to this point.  This hasn't been the case in the zombie defense mode, which seems to be FAR more difficult than the samurai mode.  I'm not really sure why this is, perhaps I'm simply playing it wrong, but many of the units in that mode seem to be stronger and harder to deal with, almost requiring the player to use charms just to stand a chance of winning.<br /><br />It also remains to be seen if I will be able to continue to perform as well as I am in the regular game as things progress.  It may be that the further I get in the game the more likely it is that I will have to purchase one of these 'special' upgrades.<br /><br />I can't really begrudge the gamemakers for this, after all, they have to make money somehow, but it's still frustrating, and and ever-present reminder of the facebook-game method of nickel and diming players for all their worth that's become ever prevalent.<br /><br />These grumps aside, this game is a lot of fun, and at the low, low, price of $0 well worth a download.<br /><br />Grab it for your Kindle and go kill some zombies!
